KING SOLOMON 'S MINES 


 by 

 H. RIDER HAGGARD 



  DEDICATION 

  this faithful but unpretending record 
  of a remarkable adventure 
  be hereby respectfully dedicate 
  by the narrator , 

  ALLAN QUATERMAIN , 

  to all the big and little boy 
  who read it . 





 AUTHOR 's NOTE 

  the author venture to take this opportunity to thank his reader 
  for the kind reception they have accord to the successive 
  edition of this tale during the last twelve year . he hop that 
  in its present form it will fall into the hand of an even wide 
  public , and that in year to come it may continue to afford 
  amusement to those who be still young enough at heart to love a 
  story of treasure , war , and wild adventure . 

  Ditchingham , 
  11 March , 1898 . 



 POST SCRIPTUM 

  now , in 1907 , on the occasion of the issue of this edition , I can 
  only add how glad I be that my romance should continue to please 
  so many reader . imagination have be verify by fact ; the King 
  Solomon 's Mines I dream of have be discover , and be put 
  out their gold once more , and , accord to the late report , 
  their diamond also ; the Kukuanas or , rather , the Matabele , have 
  be tame by the white man 's bullet , but still there seem to be 
  many who find pleasure in these simple page . that they may 
  continue so to do , even to the third and fourth generation , or 
  perhaps long still , would , I be sure , be the hope of our old and 
  depart friend , Allan Quatermain . 

  H. Rider Haggard . 
  Ditchingham , 1907 . 



 introduction 

 now that this book be print , and about to be give to the world , a 
 sense of its shortcoming both in style and content , weigh very 
 heavily upon I . as regard the latter , I can only say that it do not 
 pretend to be a full account of everything we do and see . there be 
 many thing connect with our journey into Kukuanaland that I should 
 have like to dwell upon at length , which , as it be , have be scarcely 
 allude to . amongst these be the curious legend which I collect 
 about the chain armour that save we from destruction in the great 
 battle of Loo , and also about the " Silent Ones " or Colossi at the mouth 
 of the stalactite cave . again , if I have give way to my own impulse , I 
 should have wish to go into the difference , some of which be to my 
 mind very suggestive , between the Zulu and Kukuana dialect . also a few 
 page might have be give up profitably to the consideration of the 
 indigenous flora and fauna of Kukuanaland . 1   then there remain the 
 most interesting subject - that , as it be , have only be touch on 
 incidentally - of the magnificent system of military organisation in 
 force in that country , which , in my opinion , be much superior to that 
 inaugurate by Chaka in Zululand , inasmuch as it permit of even more 
 rapid mobilisation , and do not necessitate the employment of the 
 pernicious system of enforce celibacy . lastly , I have scarcely speak 
 of the domestic and family custom of the Kukuanas , many of which be 
 exceedingly quaint , or of their proficiency in the art of smelting and 
 weld metal . this science they carry to considerable perfection , of 
 which a good example be to be see in their " tolla , " or heavy throwing 
 knife , the back of these weapon be make of hammer iron , and the 
 edge of beautiful steel weld with great skill on to the iron frame . 
 the fact of the matter be , I think , with Sir Henry Curtis and Captain 
 Good , that the good plan would be to tell my story in a plain , 
 straightforward manner , and to leave these matter to be deal with 
 subsequently in whatever way ultimately may appear to be desirable . in 
 the meanwhile I shall , of course , be delighted to give all information 
 in my power to anybody interested in such thing . 

 and now it only remain for I to offer apology for my blunt way of 
 writing . I can but say in excuse of it that I be more accustomed to 
 handle a rifle than a pen , and can not make any pretence to the grand 
 literary flight and flourish which I see in novel - for sometimes I 
 like to read a novel . I suppose they - the flight and flourish - be 
 desirable , and I regret not be able to supply they ; but at the same 
 time I can not help think that simple thing be always the most 
 impressive , and that book be easy to understand when they be 
 write in plain language , though perhaps I have no right to set up an 
 opinion on such a matter . " a sharp spear , " run the Kukuana say , 
 " need no polish " ; and on the same principle I venture to hope that a 
 true story , however strange it may be , do not require to be deck 
 out in fine word . 

 Allan Quatermain .

 the most part of the bulbous tribe . - A.Q.

 KING SOLOMON 'S MINES 


 CHAPTER I 

 I meet SIR HENRY CURTIS 

 it be a curious thing that at my age - fifty - five last birthday - I 
 should find myself take up a pen to try to write a history . I wonder 
 what sort of a history it will be when I have finish it , if ever I 
 come to the end of the trip ! I have do a good many thing in my life , 
 which seem a long one to I , owe to my have begin work so young , 
 perhaps . at an age when other boy be at school I be earn my 
 living as a trader in the old Colony . I have be trading , hunt , 
 fighting , or mining ever since . and yet it be only eight month ago 
 that I make my pile . it be a big pile now that I have get it - I do not 
 yet know how big - but I do not think I would go through the last 
 fifteen or sixteen month again for it ; no , not if I know that I should 
 come out safe at the end , pile and all . but then I be a timid man , and 
 dislike violence ; moreover , I be almost sick of adventure . I wonder why 
 I be go to write this book : it be not in my line . I be not a 
 literary man , though very devoted to the Old Testament and also to the 
 " Ingoldsby legend . " let I try to set down my reason , just to see if 
 I have any . 

 first reason : because Sir Henry Curtis and Captain John Good ask I . 

 second reason : because I be lay up here at Durban with the pain in my 
 left leg . ever since that confound lion get hold of I I have be 
 liable to this trouble , and be rather bad just now , it make I limp 
 more than ever . there must be some poison in a lion 's tooth , otherwise 
 how be it that when your wound be heal they break out again , 
 generally , mark you , at the same time of year that you get your 
 mauling ? it be a hard thing when one have shoot sixty - five lion or more , 
 as I have in the course of my life , that the sixty - sixth should chew 
 your leg like a quid of tobacco . it break the routine of the thing , 
 and put other consideration aside , I be an orderly man and do not 
 like that . this be by the way . 

 third reason : because I want my boy Harry , who be over there at the 
 hospital in London study to become a doctor , to have something to 
 amuse he and keep he out of mischief for a week or so . hospital work 
 must sometimes pall and grow rather dull , for even of cut up dead 
 body there may come satiety , and as this history will not be dull , 
 whatever else it may be , it will put a little life into thing for a 
 day or two while Harry be read of our adventure . 

 fourth reason and last : because I be go to tell the strange story 
 that I remember . it may seem a queer thing to say , especially 
 consider that there be no woman in it - except Foulata . stop , though ! 
 there be Gagaoola , if she be a woman , and not a fiend . but she be a 
 hundred at least , and therefore not marriageable , so I do not   count she . 
 at any rate , I can safely say that there be not a petticoat in the 
 whole history . 

 well , I have well come to the yoke . it be a stiff place , and I feel as 
 though I be bogge up to the axle . but , " sutjes , sutjes , " as the 
 boer say - I be sure I do not   know how they spell it - softly do it . a 
 strong team will come through at last , that is , if they be not too 
 poor . you can never do anything with poor oxen . now to make a start . 

 I , Allan Quatermain , of Durban , Natal , Gentleman , make oath and 
 say - that be how I head my deposition before the magistrate about poor 
 Khiva 's and ventv"gel 's sad death ; but somehow it do not   seem quite 
 the right way to begin a book . and , besides , be I a gentleman ? what be 
 a gentleman ? I do not   quite know , and yet I have have to do with 
 nigger - no , I will scratch out that word " nigger , " for I do not like 
 it . I have   know native who be , and so you will say , Harry , my boy , 
 before you have do with this tale , and I have know mean white with 
 lot of money and fresh out from home , too , who be not . 

 at any rate , I be bear a gentleman , though I have be nothing but a 
 poor travel trader and hunter all my life . whether I have remain 
 so I know not , you must judge of that . Heaven know I have   try . I have 
 kill many man in my time , yet I have never slay wantonly or stain 
 my hand in innocent blood , but only in self - defence . the Almighty give 
 we our life , and I suppose he mean we to defend they , at least I have 
 always act on that , and I hope it will not be bring up against I 
 when my clock strike . there , there , it be a cruel and a wicked world , 
 and for a timid man I have be mix up in a great deal of fighting . I 
 can not tell the right of it , but at any rate I have never steal , 
 though once I cheat a Kafir out of a herd of cattle . but then he have 
 do I a dirty turn , and it have trouble I ever since into the 
 bargain . 


 well , it be eighteen month or so ago since first I meet Sir Henry 
 Curtis and Captain Good . it be in this way . I have be up elephant 
 hunting beyond Bamangwato , and have meet with bad luck . everything go 
 wrong that trip , and to top up with I get the fever badly . so soon as I 
 be well enough I trek down to the Diamond Fields , sell such ivory 
 as I have , together with my wagon and oxen , discharge my hunter , and 
 take the post - cart to the cape . after spend a week in Cape Town , 
 find that they overcharge I at the hotel , and have see 
 everything there be to see , include the botanical garden , which 
 seem to I likely to confer a great benefit on the country , and the new 
 Houses of Parliament , which I expect will do nothing of the sort , I 
 determine to go back to Natal by the Dunkeld , then lie at the 
 dock wait for the Edinburgh Castle due in from England . I take my 
 berth and go aboard , and that afternoon the Natal passenger from the 
  Edinburgh Castle tranship , and we weigh and put to sea . 

 among these passenger who come on board be two who excite my 
 curiosity . one , a gentleman of about thirty , be perhaps the 
 biggest - cheste and long - armed man I ever see . he have yellow hair , a 
 thick yellow beard , clear - cut feature , and large grey eye set deep in 
 his head . I never see a finer - looking man , and somehow he remind I 
 of an ancient dane . not that I know much of ancient dane , though I 
 know a modern dane who do I out of ten pound ; but I remember once 
 see a picture of some of those gentry , who , I take it , be a kind 
 of white Zulus . they be drink out of big horn , and their long 
 hair hang down their back . as I look at my friend stand there by 
 the companion - ladder , I think that if he only let his grow a little , 
 put one of those chain shirt on to his great shoulder , and take hold 
 of a battle - axe and a horn mug , he might have sit as a model for that 
 picture . and by the way it be a curious thing , and just show how the 
 blood will out , I discover afterwards that Sir Henry Curtis , for that 
 be the big man 's name , be of danish blood . 1   he also remind I 
 strongly of somebody else , but at the time I could not remember who it 
 be . 

 the other man , who stand talk to Sir Henry , be stout and dark , and 
 of quite a different cut . I suspect at once that he be a naval 
 officer ; I do not   know why , but it be difficult to mistake a navy man . I 
 have go shoot trip with several of they in the course of my life , 
 and they have always prove themselves the good and brave and nice 
 fellow I ever meet , though sadly give , some of they , to the use of 
 profane language . I ask a page or two back , what be a gentleman ? I will 
 answer the question now : a royal naval officer be , in a general sort of 
 way , though of course there may be a black sheep among they here and 
 there . I fancy it be just the wide sea and the breath of God 's wind 
 that wash their heart and blow the bitterness out of their mind and 
 make they what man ought to be . 

 well , to return , I prove right again ; I ascertain that the dark man 
  be a naval officer , a lieutenant of thirty - one , who , after seventeen 
 year ' service , have be turn out of her Majesty 's employ with the 
 barren honour of a commander 's rank , because it be impossible that he 
 should be promote . this be what people who serve the Queen have to 
 expect : to be shoot out into the cold world to find a living just when 
 they be begin really to understand their work , and to reach the 
 prime of life . I suppose they do not   mind it , but for my own part I have 
 rather earn my bread as a hunter . one 's halfpence be as scarce 
 perhaps , but you do not get so many kick . 

 the officer 's name I find out - by refer to the passenger ' 
 list - be Good - captain John Good . he be broad , of medium height , 
 dark , stout , and rather a curious man to look at . he be so very neat 
 and so very clean - shave , and he always wear an eye - glass in his right 
 eye . it seem to grow there , for it have no string , and he never take 
 it out except to wipe it . at first I think he use to sleep in it , 
 but afterwards I find that this be a mistake . he put it in his 
 trouser pocket when he go to bed , together with his false tooth , of 
 which he have two beautiful set that , my own be none of the good , 
 have often cause I to break the tenth commandment . but I be 
 anticipate . 

 soon after we have get under way evening close in , and bring with it 
 very dirty weather . a keen breeze spring up off land , and a kind of 
 aggravate Scotch mist soon drive everybody from the deck . as for the 
  Dunkeld , she be a flat - bottomed punt , and go up light as she be , 
 she roll very heavily . it almost seem as though she would go right 
 over , but she never do . it be quite impossible to walk about , so I 
 stand near the engine where it be warm , and amuse myself with 
 watch the pendulum , which be fix opposite to I , swing slowly 
 backwards and forwards as the vessel roll , and mark the angle she 
 touch at each lurch . 

 " that pendulum 's wrong ; it be not properly weight , " suddenly say a 
 somewhat testy voice at my shoulder . look round I see the naval 
 officer whom I have notice when the passenger come aboard . 

 " indeed , now what make you think so ? " I ask . 

 " think so . I do not   think at all . why there " - as she right herself 
 after a roll - " if the ship have really roll to the degree that thing 
 point to , then she would never have roll again , that be all . but it 
 be just like these merchant skipper , they be always so confoundedly 
 careless . " 

 just then the dinner - bell rang , and I be not sorry , for it be a 
 dreadful thing to have to listen to an officer of the Royal Navy when 
 he get on to that subject . I only know one bad thing , and that be to 
 hear a merchant skipper express his candid opinion of officer of the 
 Royal Navy . 

 Captain Good and I go down to dinner together , and there we find Sir 
 Henry Curtis already seat . he and Captain Good be place together , 
 and I sit opposite to they . the captain and I soon fall into talk about 
 shooting and what not ; he ask I many question , for he be very 
 inquisitive about all sort of thing , and I answer they as well as 
 I could . presently he get on to elephant . 

 " ah , sir , " call out somebody who be sit near I , " you have   reach 
 the right man for that ; Hunter Quatermain should be able to tell you 
 about elephant if anybody can . " 

 Sir Henry , who have be sit quite quiet listen to our talk , 
 start visibly . 

 " excuse I , sir , " he say , lean forward across the table , and 
 speak in a low deep voice , a very suitable voice , it seem to I , 
 to come out of those great lung . " excuse I , sir , but be your name 
 Allan Quatermain ? " 

 I say that it be . 

 the big man make no further remark , but I hear he mutter " fortunate " 
 into his beard . 

 presently dinner come to an end , and as we be leave the saloon Sir 
 Henry stroll up and ask I if I would come into his cabin to smoke 
 a pipe . I accept , and he lead the way to the Dunkeld deck cabin , and 
 a very good cabin it be . it have be two cabin , but when Sir Garnet 
 Wolseley or one of those big swell go down the coast in the 
  Dunkeld , they knock away the partition and have never put it up 
 again . there be a sofa in the cabin , and a little table in front of 
 it . Sir Henry send the steward for a bottle of whisky , and the three of 
 we sit down and light our pipe . 

 " Mr. Quatermain , " say Sir Henry Curtis , when the man have bring the 
 whisky and light the lamp , " the year before last about this time , you 
 be , I believe , at a place call Bamangwato , to the north of the 
 Transvaal . " 

 " I be , " I answer , rather surprised that this gentleman should be so 
 well acquainted with my movement , which be not , so far as I be 
 aware , consider of general interest . 

 " you be trade there , be you not ? " put in Captain Good , in his 
 quick way . 

 " I be . I take up a wagon - load of good , make a camp outside the 
 settlement , and stop till I have sell they . " 

 Sir Henry be sit opposite to I in a Madeira chair , his arm 
 lean on the table . he now look up , fix his large grey eye full 
 upon my face . there be a curious anxiety in they , I think . 

 " do you happen to meet a man call Neville there ? " 

 " oh , yes ; he outspan alongside of I for a fortnight to rest his 
 oxen before go on to the interior . I have a letter from a lawyer a 
 few month back , ask I if I know what have become of he , which I 
 answer to the good of my ability at the time . " 

 " yes , " say Sir Henry , " your letter be forward to I . you say in it 
 that the gentleman call Neville leave Bamangwato at the beginning of 
 May in a wagon with a driver , a voorlooper , and a Kafir hunter call 
 Jim , announce his intention of trekking if possible as far as Inyati , 
 the extreme trading post in the Matabele country , where he would sell 
 his wagon and proceed on foot . you also say that he do sell his 
 wagon , for six month afterwards you see the wagon in the possession of 
 a portuguese trader , who tell you that he have buy it at Inyati from 
 a white man whose name he have forget , and that he believe the white 
 man with the native servant have start off for the interior on a 
 shooting trip . " 

 " yes . " 

 then come a pause . 

 " Mr. Quatermain , " say Sir Henry suddenly , " I suppose you know or can 
 guess nothing more of the reason of my - of Mr. Neville 's journey to 
 the northward , or as to what point that journey be direct ? " 

 " I hear something , " I answer , and stop . the subject be one which 
 I do not care to discuss . 

 Sir Henry and Captain Good look at each other , and Captain Good 
 nod . 

 " Mr. Quatermain , " go on the former , " I be go to tell you a story , 
 and ask your advice , and perhaps your assistance . the agent who 
 forward I your letter tell I that I might rely on it implicitly , as 
 you be , " he say , " well know and universally respect in Natal , and 
 especially note for your discretion . " 

 I bow and drink some whisky and water to hide my confusion , for I be 
 a modest man - and Sir Henry go on . 

 " Mr. Neville be my brother . " 

 " oh , " I say , start , for now I know of whom Sir Henry have remind 
 I when first I see he . his brother be a much small man and have a 
 dark beard , but now that I think of it , he possess eye of the same 
 shade of grey and with the same keen look in they : the feature too 
 be not unlike . 

 " he be , " go on Sir Henry , " my only and young brother , and till 
 five year ago I do not suppose that we be ever a month away from 
 each other . but just about five year ago a misfortune befall we , as 
 sometimes do happen in family . we quarrel bitterly , and I 
 behave unjustly to my brother in my anger . " 

 here Captain Good nod his head vigorously to himself . the ship give 
 a big roll just then , so that the look - glass , which be fix 
 opposite we to starboard , be for a moment nearly over our head , and 
 as I be sit with my hand in my pocket and star upwards , I 
 could see he nod like anything . 

 " as I daresay you know , " go on Sir Henry , " if a man die intestate , 
 and have no property but land , real property it be call in England , it 
 all descend to his eld son . it so happen that just at the time 
 when we quarrel our father die intestate . he have put off make his 
 will until it be too late . the result be that my brother , who have not 
 be bring up to any profession , be leave without a penny . of course 
 it would have be my duty to provide for he , but at the time the 
 quarrel between we be so bitter that I do not - to my shame I say it 
  and he sigh deeply   - offer to do anything . it be not that I grudge 
 him justice , but I wait for he to make advance , and he make none . I 
 be sorry to trouble you with all this , Mr. Quatermain , but I must to 
 make thing clear , eh , good ? " 

 " quite so , quite so , " say the captain . " Mr. Quatermain will , I be 
 sure , keep this history to himself . " 

 " of course , " say I , for I rather pride myself on my discretion , for 
 which , as Sir Henry have hear , I have some repute . 

 " well , " go on Sir Henry , " my brother have a few hundred pound to his 
 account at the time . without say anything to I he draw out this 
 paltry sum , and , have adopt the name of Neville , start off for 
 South Africa in the wild hope of make a fortune . this I learn 
 afterwards . some three year pass , and I hear nothing of my brother , 
 though I write several time . doubtless the letter never reach he . 
 but as time go on I grow more and more troubled about he . I find 
 out , Mr. Quatermain , that blood be thick than water . " 

 " that be true , " say I , think of my boy Harry . 

 " I find out , Mr. Quatermain , that I would have give half my fortune 
 to know that my brother George , the only relation I possess , be safe 
 and well , and that I should see he again . " 

 " but you never do , Curtis , " jerk out Captain Good , glance at the 
 big man 's face . 

 " well , Mr. Quatermain , as time go on I become more and more anxious 
 to find out if my brother be alive or dead , and if alive to get he 
 home again . I set enquiry on foot , and your letter be one of the 
 result . so far as it go it be satisfactory , for it show that till 
 lately George be alive , but it do not go far enough . so , to cut a 
 long story short , I make up my mind to come out and look for he 
 myself , and Captain Good be so kind as to come with I . " 

 " yes , " say the captain ; " nothing else to do , you see . turn out by my 
 Lords of the Admiralty to starve on half pay . and now perhaps , sir , you 
 will tell we what you know or have hear of the gentleman call 
 Neville . "

 CHAPTER II 

 the LEGEND of SOLOMON 'S MINES 

 " what be it that you hear about my brother 's journey at Bamangwato ? " 
 ask Sir Henry , as I pause to fill my pipe before reply to Captain 
 Good . 

 " I hear this , " I answer , " and I have never mention it to a soul 
 till to - day . I hear that he be start for Solomon 's Mines . " 

 " Solomon 's Mines ? " ejaculate both my hearer at once . " where be they ? " 

 " I do not   know , " I say ; " I know where they be say to be . once I see 
 the peak of the mountain that border they , but there be a hundred 
 and thirty mile of desert between I and they , and I be not aware that 
 any white man ever get across it save one . but perhaps the good thing I 
 can do be to tell you the legend of Solomon 's Mines as I know it , you 
 pass your word not to reveal anything I tell you without my 
 permission . do you agree to that ? I have my reason for ask . " 

 Sir Henry nod , and Captain Good reply , " certainly , certainly . " 

 " well , " I begin , " as you may guess , generally speak , elephant 
 hunter be a rough set of man , who do not trouble themselves with much 
 beyond the fact of life and the way of kafir . but here and there you 
 meet a man who take the trouble to collect tradition from the 
 native , and try to make out a little piece of the history of this 
 dark land . it be such a man as this who first tell I the legend of 
 Solomon 's Mines , now a matter of nearly thirty year ago . that be when 
 I be on my first elephant hunt in the Matabele country . his name be 
 Evans , and he be kill the following year , poor fellow , by a wounded 
 buffalo , and lie bury near the Zambesi Falls . I be tell Evans 
 one night , I remember , of some wonderful working I have find whilst 
 hunt koodoo and eland in what be now the Lydenburg district of the 
 Transvaal . I see they have come across these working again lately in 
 prospect for gold , but I know of they year ago . there be a great 
 wide wagon road cut out of the solid rock , and lead to the mouth of 
 the working or gallery . inside the mouth of this gallery be stack of 
 gold quartz pile up ready for roasting , which show that the worker , 
 whoever they be , must have leave in a hurry . also , about twenty pace 
 in , the gallery be build across , and a beautiful bit of masonry it be . " 

 " ' ay , ' say Evans , ' but I will spin you a queerer yarn than that ' ; and 
 he go on to tell I how he have find in the far interior a ruin 
 city , which he believe to be the Ophir of the Bible , and , by the way , 
 other more learned man have say the same long since poor Evans 's time . 
 I be , I remember , listen open - eared to all these wonder , for I be 
 young at the time , and this story of an ancient civilisation and of the 
 treasure which those old jewish or phoenician adventurer use to 
 extract from a country long since lapse into the dark barbarism 
 take a great hold upon my imagination , when suddenly he say to I , 
 ' Lad , do you ever hear of the Suliman Mountains up to the north - west 
 of the Mushakulumbwe country ? ' I tell he I never have . ' ah , well , ' he 
 say , ' that be where Solomon really have his mine , his diamond mine , I 
 mean . ' 

 " ' how do you know that ? ' I ask . 

 " ' know it ! why , what be " Suliman " but a corruption of Solomon ? 1 
 besides , an old Isanusi or witch doctoress up in the Manica country 
 tell I all about it . she say that the people who live across those 
 mountain be a " branch " of the Zulus , speak a dialect of Zulu , but 
 fine and big man even ; that there live among they great wizard , 
 who have learn their art from white man when " all the world be dark , " 
 and who have the secret of a wonderful mine of " bright stone . " ' 

 " well , I laugh at this story at the time , though it interest I , 
 for the Diamond Fields be not discover then , but poor Evans go 
 off and be kill , and for twenty year I never think any more of 
 the matter . however , just twenty year afterwards - and that be a long 
 time , gentleman ; an elephant hunter do not often live for twenty 
 year at his business - I hear something more definite about Suliman 's 
 Mountains and the country which lie beyond they . I be up beyond the 
 Manica country , at a place call Sitanda 's Kraal , and a miserable 
 place it be , for a man could get nothing to eat , and there be but 
 little game about . I have an attack of fever , and be in a bad way 
 generally , when one day a Portugee arrive with a single companion - a 
 half - breed . now I know your low - class Delagoa Portugee well . there be 
 no great devil unhung in a general way , batten as he do upon 
 human agony and flesh in the shape of slave . but this be quite a 
 different type of man to the mean fellow whom I have be accustom to 
 meet ; indeed , in appearance he remind I more of the polite dom I 
 have read about , for he be tall and thin , with large dark eye and 
 curl grey mustachio . we talk together for a while , for he could 
 speak break English , and I understand a little Portugee , and he tell 
 I that his name be José Silvestre , and that he have a place near 
 Delagoa Bay . when he go on next day with his half - breed companion , he 
 say ' good - bye , ' take off his hat quite in the old style . 

 " ' good - bye , senor , ' he say ; ' if ever we meet again I shall be the 
 rich man in the world , and I will remember you . ' I laugh a 
 little - I be too weak to laugh much - and watch he strike out for 
 the great desert to the west , wonder if he be mad , or what he 
 think he be go to find there . 

 " a week pass , and I get the well of my fever . one evening I be 
 sit on the ground in front of the little tent I have with I , 
 chew the last leg of a miserable fowl I have buy from a native for 
 a bit of cloth worth twenty fowl , and star at the hot red sun 
 sink down over the desert , when suddenly I see a figure , apparently 
 that of a European , for it wear a coat , on the slope of the rise 
 ground opposite to I , about three hundred yard away . the figure creep 
 along on its hand and knee , then it get up and stagger forward a 
 few yard on its leg , only to fall and crawl again . see that it 
 must be somebody in distress , I send one of my hunter to help he , and 
 presently he arrive , and who do you suppose it turn out to be ? " 

 " José Silvestre , of course , " say Captain Good . 

 " yes , José Silvestre , or rather his skeleton and a little skin . his 
 face be a bright yellow with bilious fever , and his large dark eye 
 stand nearly out of his head , for all the flesh have go . there be 
 nothing but yellow parchment - like skin , white hair , and the gaunt bone 
 stick up beneath . 

 " ' Water ! for the sake of Christ , water ! ' he moan and I see that his 
 lip be crack , and his tongue , which protrude between they , be 
 swollen and blackish . 

 " I give he water with a little milk in it , and he drink it in great 
 gulp , two quart or so , without stop . I would not let he have any 
 more . then the fever take he again , and he fall down and begin to rave 
 about Suliman 's Mountains , and the diamond , and the desert . I carry 
 he into the tent and do what I could for he , which be little 
 enough ; but I see how it must end . about eleven o'clock he grow 
 quieter , and I lie down for a little rest and go to sleep . at dawn I 
 wake again , and in the half light see Silvestre sit up , a strange , 
 gaunt form , and gaze out towards the desert . presently the first ray 
 of the sun shoot right across the wide plain before we till it reach 
 the faraway crest of one of the tall of the Suliman Mountains more 
 than a hundred mile away . 

 " ' there it be ! ' cry the die man in Portuguese , and point with 
 his long , thin arm , ' but I shall never reach it , never . no one will 
 ever reach it ! ' 

 " suddenly , he pause , and seem to take a resolution . ' friend , ' he 
 say , turn towards I , ' be you there ? my eye grow dark . ' 

 " ' yes , ' I say ; ' yes , lie down now , and rest . ' 

 " ' ay , ' he answer , ' I shall rest soon , I have time to rest - all 
 eternity . listen , I be die ! you have be good to I . I will give you 
 the writing . perhaps you will get there if you can live to pass the 
 desert , which have kill my poor servant and I . ' 

 " then he grope in his shirt and bring out what I think be a boer 
 tobacco pouch make of the skin of the Swart - vet - pen or sable antelope . 
 it be fasten with a little strip of hide , what we call a rimpi , and 
 this he try to loose , but could not . he hand it to I . ' untie it , ' 
 he say . I do so , and extract a bit of tear yellow linen on which 
 something be write in rusty letter . inside this rag be a paper . 

 " then he go on feebly , for he be grow weak : ' the paper have all 
 that be on the linen . it take I year to read . listen : my ancestor , a 
 political refugee from Lisbon , and one of the first Portuguese who 
 land on these shore , write that when he be die on those mountain 
 which no white foot ever press before or since . his name be José da 
 Silvestra , and he live three hundred year ago . his slave , who wait 
 for he on this side of the mountain , find he dead , and bring the 
 writing home to Delagoa . it have be in the family ever since , but none 
 have care to read it , till at last I do . and I have lose my life over 
 it , but another may succeed , and become the rich man in the 
 world - the rich man in the world . only give it to no one , senor ; go 
 yourself ! ' 

 " then he begin to wander again , and in an hour it be all over . 

 " God rest he ! he die very quietly , and I bury he deep , with big 
 boulder on his breast ; so I do not think that the jackal can have dig 
 he up . and then I come away . " 

 " ay , but the document ? " say Sir Henry , in a tone of deep interest . 

 " yes , the document ; what be in it ? " add the captain . 

 " well , gentleman , if you like I will tell you . I have never show it 
 to anybody yet except to a drunken old portuguese trader who translate 
 it for I , and have forget all about it by the next morning . the 
 original rag be at my home in Durban , together with poor Dom José 's 
 translation , but I have the English rendering in my pocket - book , and a 
 facsimile of the map , if it can be call a map . here it be . " 

  Illustration : MAP 

  " I , José da Silvestra , who be now die of hunger in the little 
  cave where no snow be on the north side of the nipple of the 
  southernmost of the two mountain I have name Sheba 's Breasts , 
  write this in the year 1590 with a cleft bone upon a remnant of my 
  raiment , my blood be the ink . if my slave should find it when 
  he come , and should bring it to Delagoa , let my friend   name 
  illegible   bring the matter to the knowledge of the king , that he 
  may send an army which , if they live through the desert and the 
  mountain , and can overcome the brave Kukuanes and their devilish 
  art , to which end many priest should be bring , will make he 
  the rich king since Solomon . with my own eye I have see the 
  countless diamond store in Solomon 's treasure chamber behind the 
  white death ; but through the treachery of Gagool the witch - finder 
  I might bring nought away , scarcely my life . let he who come 
  follow the map , and climb the snow of Sheba 's left breast till he 
  reach the nipple , on the north side of which be the great road 
  Solomon make , from whence three day ' journey to the King 's 
  Palace . let he kill Gagool . pray for my soul . farewell . 

 José da Silvestra . " 2 


 when I have finish read the above , and show the copy of the map , 
 draw by the die hand of the old Dom with his blood for ink , there 
 follow a silence of astonishment . 

 " well , " say Captain Good , " I have be round the world twice , and put 
 in at most port , but may I be hang for a mutineer if ever I hear a 
 yarn like this out of a story book , or in it either , for the matter of 
 that . " 

 " it be a queer tale , Mr. Quatermain , " say Sir Henry . " I suppose you be 
 not hoax we ? it be , I know , sometimes think allowable to take in a 
 greenhorn . " 

 " if you think that , Sir Henry , " I say , much put out , and pocket my 
 paper - for I do not like to be think one of those silly fellow who 
 consider it witty to tell lie , and who be for ever boast to 
 newcomer of extraordinary hunting adventure which never happen - " if 
 you think that , why , there be an end to the matter , " and I rise to go . 

 Sir Henry lay his large hand upon my shoulder . " sit down , Mr. 
 Quatermain , " he say , " I beg your pardon ; I see very well you do not 
 wish to deceive we , but the story sound so strange that I could 
 hardly believe it . " 

 " you shall see the original map and write when we reach Durban , " I 
 answer , somewhat mollify , for really when I come to consider the 
 question it be scarcely wonderful that he should doubt my good faith . 

 " but , " I go on , " I have not tell you about your brother . I know the 
 man Jim who be with he . he be a Bechuana by birth , a good hunter , 
 and for a native a very clever man . that morning on which Mr. Neville 
 be start I see Jim stand by my wagon and cut up tobacco on 
 the disselboom . 

 " ' Jim , ' say I , ' where be you off to this trip ? it be elephant ? ' 

 " ' no , Baas , ' he answer , ' we be after something worth much more than 
 ivory . ' 

 " ' and what might that be ? ' I say , for I be curious . ' be it gold ? ' 

 " ' no , Baas , something worth more than gold , ' and he grin . 

 " I ask no more question , for I do not like to lower my dignity by 
 seem inquisitive , but I be puzzle . presently Jim finish cut 
 his tobacco . 

 " ' baas , ' say he . 

 " I take no notice . 

 " ' baas , ' say he again . 

 " ' eh , boy , what be it ? ' I ask . 

 " ' Baas , we be go after diamond . ' 

 " ' diamond ! why , then , you be steer in the wrong direction ; you 
 should head for the Fields . ' 

 " ' Baas , have you ever hear of Suliman 's Berg ? ' - that be , Solomon 's 
 Mountains , Sir Henry . 

 " ' ay ! ' 

 " ' have you ever hear of the diamond there ? ' 

 " ' I have hear a foolish story , Jim . ' 

 " ' it be no story , Baas . once I know a woman who come from there , and 
 reach Natal with her child , she tell I : - she be dead now . ' 

 " ' your master will feed the assv"gel ' - that is , vulture - ' Jim , if he 
 try to reach Suliman 's country , and so will you if they can get any 
 picking off your worthless old carcass , ' say I. 

 " he grin . ' Mayhap , Baas . man must die ; I would rather like to try a new 
 country myself ; the elephant be get work out about here . ' 

 " ' ah ! my boy , ' I say , ' you wait till the " pale old man " get a grip of 
 your yellow throat , and then we shall hear what sort of a tune you 
 sing . ' 

 " half an hour after that I see Neville 's wagon move off . presently Jim 
 come back run . ' good - bye , Baas , ' he say . ' I do not   like to start 
 without bid you good - bye , for I daresay you be right , and that we 
 shall never trek south again . ' 

 " ' be your master really go to Suliman 's Berg , Jim , or be you lie ? ' 

 " ' no , ' he answer , ' he be go . he tell I he be bind to make his 
 fortune somehow , or try to ; so he might as well have a fling for the 
 diamond . ' 

 " ' oh ! ' I say ; ' wait a bit , Jim ; will you take a note to your master , 
 Jim , and promise not to give it to he till you reach Inyati ? ' which 
 be some hundred mile off . 

 " ' yes , Baas . ' 

 " so I take a scrap of paper , and write on it , ' let he who come . . . 
 climb the snow of Sheba 's left breast , till he reach the nipple , on 
 the north side of which be Solomon 's great road . ' 

 " ' now , Jim , ' I say , ' when you give this to your master , tell he he 
 have well follow the advice on it implicitly . you be not to give it 
 to he now , because I do not   want he back ask I question which I 
 will not   answer . now be off , you idle fellow , the wagon be nearly out of 
 sight . ' 

 " Jim take the note and go , and that be all I know about your brother , 
 Sir Henry ; but I be much afraid - " 

 " Mr. Quatermain , " say Sir Henry , " I be go to look for my brother ; I 
 be go to trace he to Suliman 's Mountains , and over they if 
 necessary , till I find he , or until I know that he be dead . will you 
 come with I ? " 

 I be , as I think I have say , a cautious man , indeed a timid one , and 
 this suggestion frighten I . it seem to I that to undertake such a 
 journey would be to go to certain death , and put other 
 consideration aside , as I have a son to support , I could not afford to 
 die just then . 

 " no , thank you , Sir Henry , I think I have rather not , " I answer . " I be 
 too old for wild - goose chase of that sort , and we should only end up 
 like my poor friend Silvestre . I have a son dependent on I , so I 
 can not afford to risk my life foolishly . " 

 both Sir Henry and Captain Good look very disappointed . 

 " Mr. Quatermain , " say the former , " I be well off , and I be bent upon 
 this business . you may put the remuneration for your service at 
 whatever figure you like in reason , and it shall be pay over to you 
 before we start . moreover , I will arrange in the event of anything 
 untoward happen to we or to you , that your son shall be suitably 
 provide for . you will see from this offer how necessary I think your 
 presence . also if by chance we should reach this place , and find 
 diamond , they shall belong to you and good equally . I do not want 
 they . but of course that promise be worth nothing at all , though the 
 same thing would apply to any ivory we might get . you may pretty well 
 make your own term with I , Mr. Quatermain ; and of course I shall pay 
 all expense . " 

 " Sir Henry , " say I , " this be the most liberal proposal I ever have , and 
 one not to be sneeze at by a poor hunter and trader . but the job be 
 the big I have come across , and I must take time to think it over . 
 I will give you my answer before we get to Durban . " 

 " very good , " answer Sir Henry . 

 then I say good - night and turn in , and dream about poor long - dead 
 Silvestre and the diamond .

 CHAPTER III 

 umbopa enter our service 

 it take from four to five day , accord to the speed of the vessel 
 and the state of the weather , to run up from the Cape to Durban . 
 sometimes , if the landing be bad at East London , where they have not 
 yet make that wonderful harbour they talk so much of , and sink such a 
 mint of money in , a ship be delay for twenty - four hour before the 
 cargo boat can get out to take off the good . but on this occasion we 
 have not to wait at all , for there be no breaker on the Bar to speak 
 of , and the tug come out at once with the long string of ugly 
 flat - bottom boat behind they , into which the package be bundle 
 with a crash . it do not matter what they might be , over they go 
 slap - bang ; whether they contain china or woollen good they meet with 
 the same treatment . I see one case hold four dozen of champagne 
 smash all to bit , and there be the champagne fizzing and boiling 
 about in the bottom of the dirty cargo boat . it be a wicked waste , and 
 evidently so the Kafirs in the boat think , for they find a couple of 
 unbroken bottle , and knock off the neck drink the content . but 
 they have not allow for the expansion cause by the fizz in the wine , 
 and , feel themselves swell , roll about in the bottom of the 
 boat , call out that the good liquor be " tagati " - that be , 
 bewitch . I speak to they from the vessel , and tell they it be the 
 white man 's strong medicine , and that they be as good as dead man . 
 those Kafirs go to the shore in a very great fright , and I do not 
 think that they will touch champagne again . 

 well , all the time that we be steam up to Natal I be think 
 over Sir Henry Curtis 's offer . we do not speak any more on the subject 
 for a day or two , though I tell they many hunting yarn , all true one . 
 there be no need to tell lie about hunting , for so many curious thing 
 happen within the knowledge of a man whose business it be to hunt ; but 
 this be by the way . 

 at last , one beautiful evening in January , which be our hot month , 
 we steam past the coast of Natal , expect to make Durban Point by 
 sunset . it be a lovely coast all along from East London , with its red 
 sandhill and wide sweep of vivid green , dot here and there with 
 Kafir kraal , and border by a ribbon of white surf , which spout up 
 in pillar of foam where it hit the rock . but just before you come to 
 Durban there be a peculiar richness about the landscape . there be the 
 sheer kloof cut in the hill by the rush rain of century , down 
 which the river sparkle ; there be the deep green of the bush , 
 grow as God plant it , and the other green of the mealie gardens 
 and the sugar patch , while now and again a white house , smile out 
 at the placid sea , put a finish and give an air of homeliness to the 
 scene . for to my mind , however beautiful a view may be , it require the 
 presence of man to make it complete , but perhaps that be because I have 
 live so much in the wilderness , and therefore know the value of 
 civilisation , though to be sure it drive away the game . the Garden of 
 Eden , no doubt , look fair before man be , but I always think that it 
 must have be fair when Eve adorn it . 

 to return , we have miscalculate a little , and the sun be well down 
 before we drop anchor off the point , and hear the gun which tell 
 the good folk of Durban that the English Mail be in . it be too late 
 to think of get over the Bar that night , so we go comfortably to 
 dinner , after see the Mails carry off in the life - boat . 

 when we come up again the moon be out , and shin so brightly over 
 sea and shore that she almost pal the quick , large flash from the 
 lighthouse . from the shore float sweet spicy odour that always 
 remind I of hymn and missionary , and in the window of the house 
 on the Berea sparkle a hundred light . from a large brig lie near 
 also come the music of the sailor as they work at get the anchor 
 up in order to be ready for the wind . altogether it be a perfect 
 night , such a night as you sometimes get in Southern Africa , and it 
 throw a garment of peace over everybody as the moon throw a garment of 
 silver over everything . even the great bulldog , belong to a sporting 
 passenger , seem to yield to its gentle influence , and forget his 
 yearning to come to close quarter with the baboon in a cage on the 
 foc'sle , snore happily at the door of the cabin , dream no doubt 
 that he have finish he , and happy in his dream . 

 we three - that is , Sir Henry Curtis , Captain Good , and myself - go and 
 sit by the wheel , and be quiet for a while . 

 " well , Mr. Quatermain , " say Sir Henry presently , " have you be 
 think about my proposal ? " 

 " ay , " echo Captain Good , " what do you think of they , Mr. Quatermain ? 
 I hope that you be go to give we the pleasure of your company so 
 far as Solomon 's Mines , or wherever the gentleman you know as Neville 
 may have get to . " 

 I rise and knock out my pipe before I answer . I have not make up my 
 mind , and want an additional moment to decide . before the burning 
 tobacco have fall into the sea I have decide ; just that little extra 
 second do the trick . it be often the way when you have be bother 
 a long time over a thing . 

 " yes , gentleman , " I say , sit down again , " I will go , and by your 
 leave I will tell you why , and on what condition . first for the term 
 which I ask . 

 " 1 . you be to pay all expense , and any ivory or other valuable we 
 may get be to be divide between Captain Good and myself . 

 " 2 . that you give I   500 for my service on the trip before we start , 
 I undertaking to serve you faithfully till you choose to abandon the 
 enterprise , or till we succeed , or disaster overtake we . 

 " 3 . that before we trek you execute a deed agree , in the event of my 
 death or disablement , to pay my boy Harry , who be study medicine 
 over there in London , at Guy 's Hospital , a sum of   200 a year for five 
 year , by which time he ought to be able to earn a living for himself 
 if he be worth his salt . that be all , I think , and I daresay you will 
 say quite enough too . " 

 " no , " answer Sir Henry , " I accept they gladly . I be bent upon this 
 project , and would pay more than that for your help , consider the 
 peculiar and exclusive knowledge which you possess . " 

 " pity I do not ask it , then , but I will not   go back on my word . and now 
 that I have get my term I will tell you my reason for make up my 
 mind to go . first of all , gentleman , I have be observe you both for 
 the last few day , and if you will not think I impertinent I may say 
 that I like you , and believe that we shall come up well to the yoke 
 together . that be something , let I tell you , when one have a long 
 journey like this before one . 

 " and now as to the journey itself , I tell you flatly , Sir Henry and 
 Captain Good , that I do not think it probable we can come out of it 
 alive , that is , if we attempt to cross the Suliman Mountains . what be 
 the fate of the old Dom da Silvestra three hundred year ago ? what be 
 the fate of his descendant twenty year ago ? what have be your 
 brother 's fate ? I tell you frankly , gentleman , that as their fate be 
 so I believe our will be . " 

 I pause to watch the effect of my word . Captain Good look a little 
 uncomfortable , but Sir Henry 's face do not change . " we must take our 
 chance , " he say . 

 " you may perhaps wonder , " I go on , " why , if I think this , I , who be , 
 as I tell you , a timid man , should undertake such a journey . it be for 
 two reason . first I be a fatalist , and believe that my time be 
 appoint to come quite without reference to my own movement and will , 
 and that if I be to go to Suliman 's Mountains to be kill , I shall go 
 there and shall be kill . God Almighty , no doubt , know his mind about 
 I , so I need not trouble on that point . secondly , I be a poor man . for 
 nearly forty year I have hunt and trade , but I have never make more 
 than a living . well , gentleman , I do not   know if you be aware that the 
 average life of an elephant hunter from the time he take to the trade 
 be between four and five year . so you see I have live through about 
 seven generation of my class , and I should think that my time can not 
 be far off , anyway . now , if anything be to happen to I in the 
 ordinary course of business , by the time my debt be pay there would 
 be nothing leave to support my son Harry whilst he be get in the 
 way of earn a living , whereas now he will be set up for five year . 
 there be the whole affair in a nutshell . " 

 " Mr. Quatermain , " say Sir Henry , who have be give I his most 
 serious attention , " your motif for undertake an enterprise which 
 you believe can only end in disaster reflect a great deal of credit on 
 you . whether or not you be right , of course time and the event alone 
 can show . but whether you be right or wrong , I may as well tell you at 
 once that I be go through with it to the end , sweet or bitter . if we 
 be to be knock on the head , all I have to say be , that I hope we get 
 a little shooting first , eh , good ? " 

 " yes , yes , " put in the captain . " we have all three of we be 
 accustom to face danger , and to hold our life in our hand in 
 various way , so it be no good turn back now . and now I vote we go 
 down to the saloon and take an observation just for luck , you know . " 
 and we do - through the bottom of a tumbler . 

 next day we go ashore , and I put up Sir Henry and Captain Good at the 
 little shanty I have build on the Berea , and which I call my home . 
 there be only three room and a kitchen in it , and it be construct 
 of green brick with a galvanised iron roof , but there be a good garden 
 with the good loquot tree in it that I know , and some nice young 
 mango , of which I hope great thing . the curator of the botanical 
 garden give they to I . it be look after by an old hunter of mine 
 name Jack , whose thigh be so badly break by a buffalo cow in 
 Sikukunis country that he will never hunt again . but he can potter 
 about and garden , be a Griqua by birth . you will never persuade a 
 Zulu to take much interest in gardening . it be a peaceful art , and 
 peaceful art be not in his line . 

 Sir Henry and Good sleep in a tent pitch in my little grove of orange 
 tree at the end of the garden , for there be no room for they in the 
 house , and what with the smell of the bloom , and the sight of the green 
 and golden fruit - in Durban you will see all three on the tree 
 together - I daresay it be a pleasant place enough , for we have few 
 mosquito here on the Berea , unless there happen to come an unusually 
 heavy rain . 

 well , to get on - for if I do not , Harry , you will be tired of my story 
 before ever we fetch up at Suliman 's Mountains - have once make up my 
 mind to go I set about make the necessary preparation . first I 
 secure the deed from Sir Henry , provide for you , my boy , in case of 
 accident . there be some difficulty about its legal execution , as Sir 
 Henry be a stranger here , and the property to be charge be over the 
 water ; but it be ultimately get over with the help of a lawyer , who 
 charge   20 for the job - a price that I think outrageous . then I 
 pocket my cheque for   500 . 

 have pay this tribute to my bump of caution , I purchase a wagon and 
 a span of oxen on Sir Henry 's behalf , and beauty they be . it be a 
 twenty - two - foot wagon with iron axle , very strong , very light , and 
 build throughout of stink wood ; not quite a new one , have be to the 
 Diamond Fields and back , but , in my opinion , all the well for that , 
 for I could see that the wood be well seasoned . if anything be go 
 to give in a wagon , or if there be green wood in it , it will show out 
 on the first trip . this particular vehicle be what we call a 
 " half - tent " wagon , that be to say , only cover in over the after 
 twelve foot , leave all the front part free for the necessary we have 
 to carry with we . in this after part be a hide " cartle , " or bed , on 
 which two people could sleep , also rack for rifle , and many other 
 little convenience . I give   125 for it , and think that it be cheap at 
 the price . 

 then I buy a beautiful team of twenty Zulu oxen , which I have keep my 
 eye on for a year or two . sixteen oxen be the usual number for a team , 
 but I take four extra to allow for casualty . these Zulu cattle be 
 small and light , not more than half the size of the Africander oxen , 
 which be generally use for transport purpose ; but they will live 
 where the Africanders would starve , and with a moderate load can make 
 five mile a day well go , be quick and not so liable to 
 become footsore . what be more , this lot be thoroughly ' salt , " that 
 be , they have work all over South Africa , and so have become proof , 
 comparatively speak , against red water , which so frequently destroy 
 whole team of oxen when they get on to strange " veldt " or grass 
 country . as for " lung sick , " which be a dreadful form of pneumonia , 
 very prevalent in this country , they have all be inoculate against 
 it . this be do by cut a slit in the tail of an ox , and bind in 
 a piece of the diseased lung of an animal which have die of the 
 sickness . the result be that the ox sicken , take the disease in a 
 mild form , which cause its tail to drop off , as a rule about a foot 
 from the root , and become proof against future attack . it seem cruel 
 to rob the animal of his tail , especially in a country where there be 
 so many fly , but it be well to sacrifice the tail and keep the ox 
 than to lose both tail and ox , for a tail without an ox be not much 
 good , except to dust with . still it do look odd to trek along behind 
 twenty stump , where there ought to be tail . it seem as though Nature 
 make a trifling mistake , and stick the stern ornament of a lot of 
 prize bull - dog on to the rump of the oxen . 

 next come the question of provisioning and medicine , one which 
 require the most careful consideration , for what we have to do be to 
 avoid lumber the wagon , and yet to take everything absolutely 
 necessary . fortunately , it turn out that Good be a bit of a doctor , 
 have at some point in his previous career manage to pass through a 
 course of medical and surgical instruction , which he have more or less 
 keep up . he be not , of course , qualified , but he know more about it 
 than many a man who can write M.D. after his name , as we find out 
 afterwards , and he have a splendid travel medicine chest and a set 
 of instrument . whilst we be at Durban he cut off a Kafir 's big toe 
 in a way which it be a pleasure to see . but he be quite nonplussed 
 when the Kafir , who have sit stolidly watch the operation , ask he 
 to put on another , say that a " white one " would do at a pinch . 

 there remain , when these question be satisfactorily settle , two 
 far important point for consideration , namely , that of arm and 
 that of servant . as to the arm I can not do well than put down a 
 list of those which we finally decide on from among the ample store 
 that Sir Henry have bring with he from England , and those which I 
 own . I copy it from my pocket - book , where I make the entry at the 
 time . 

 " three heavy breech - load double - eight elephant gun , weigh about 
 fifteen pound each , to carry a charge of eleven drachm of black 
 powder . " two of these be by a well - know London firm , most excellent 
 maker , but I do not know by whom mine , which be not so highly 
 finish , be make . I have use it on several trip , and shoot a good 
 many elephant with it , and it have always prove a most superior 
 weapon , thoroughly to be rely on . 

 " three double-500 express , construct to stand a charge of six 
 drachm , " sweet weapon , and admirable for medium - sized game , such as 
 eland or sable antelope , or for man , especially in an open country and 
 with the semi - hollow bullet . 

 " one double no . 12 central - fire Keeper 's shot - gun , full choke both 
 barrel . " this gun prove of the great service to we afterwards in 
 shooting game for the pot . 

 " three Winchester repeating rifle   not carbine , spare gun . 

 " three single - action Colt 's revolver , with the heavy , or american 
 pattern of cartridge . " 

 this be our total armament , and doubtless the reader will observe that 
 the weapon of each class be of the same make and calibre , so that 
 the cartridge be interchangeable , a very important point . I make no 
 apology for detail it at length , as every experienced hunter will 
 know how vital a proper supply of gun and ammunition be to the success 
 of an expedition . 

 now as to the man who be to go with we . after much consultation we 
 decide that their number should be limit to five , namely , a driver , 
 a leader , and three servant . 

 the driver and leader I find without much difficulty , two Zulus , name 
 respectively Goza and Tom ; but to get the servant prove a more 
 difficult matter . it be necessary that they should be thoroughly 
 trustworthy and brave man , as in a business of this sort our life 
 might depend upon their conduct . at last I secure two , one a Hottentot 
 name Ventv"gel , or " windbird , " and one a little Zulu name Khiva , who 
 have the merit of speak English perfectly . ventv"gel I have know 
 before ; he be one of the most perfect ' spoorer , " that be , game 
 tracker , I ever have to do with , and tough as whipcord . he never seem 
 to tire . but he have one fail , so common with his race , drink . put 
 he within reach of a bottle of gin and you could not trust he . 
 however , as we be go beyond the region of grog - shop this little 
 weakness of his do not so much matter . 

 have secure these two man I look in vain for a third to suit my 
 purpose , so we determine to start without one , trust to luck to 
 find a suitable man on our way up country . but , as it happen , on the 
 evening before the day we have fix for our departure the Zulu Khiva 
 inform I that a Kafir be wait to see I . accordingly , when we 
 have do dinner , for we be at table at the time , I tell Khiva to 
 bring he in . presently a tall , handsome - look man , somewhere about 
 thirty year of age , and very light - colour for a Zulu , enter , and 
 lift his knob - stick by way of salute , squat himself down in the 
 corner on his haunch , and sit silent . I do not take any notice of 
 he for a while , for it be a great mistake to do so . if you rush into 
 conversation at once , a Zulu be apt to think you a person of little 
 dignity or consequence . I observe , however , that he be a " Keshla " or 
 ring man ; that is , he wear on his head the black ring , make of a 
 specie of gum polish with fat and work up in the hair , which be 
 usually assume by Zulus on attain a certain age or dignity . also it 
 strike I that his face be familiar to I . 

 " well , " I say at last , " what be your name ? " 

 " Umbopa , " answer the man in a slow , deep voice . 

 " I have see your face before . " 

 " yes ; the Inkoosi , the chief , my father , see my face at the place of 
 the little Hand " - that be , Isandhlwana - " on the day before the battle . " 

 then I remember . I be one of Lord Chelmsford 's guide in that 
 unlucky Zulu War , and have the good fortune to leave the camp in charge 
 of some wagon on the day before the battle . while I be wait for 
 the cattle to be inspan I fall into conversation with this man , who 
 hold some small command among the native auxiliary , and he have 
 express to I his doubt as to the safety of the camp . at the time I 
 tell he to hold his tongue , and leave such matter to wise head ; but 
 afterwards I think of his word . 

 " I remember , " I say ; " what be it you want ? " 

 " it be this , ' Macumazahn . ' " that be my Kafir name , and mean the man 
 who get up in the middle of the night , or , in vulgar English , he who 
 keep his eye open . " I hear that you go on a great expedition far into 
 the North with the white chief from over the water . be it a true word ? " 

 " it be . " 

 " I hear that you go even to the Lukanga River , a moon 's journey beyond 
 the Manica country . be this so also , ' Macumazahn ? ' " 

 " why do you ask whither we go ? what be it to you ? " I answer 
 suspiciously , for the object of our journey have be keep a dead 
 secret . 

 " it be this , o white man , that if indeed you travel so far I would 
 travel with you . " 

 there be a certain assumption of dignity in the man 's mode of speech , 
 and especially in his use of the word " o white man , " instead of " O 
 Inkosis , " or chief , which strike I . 

 " you forget yourself a little , " I say . " your word run out unaware . 
 that be not the way to speak . what be your name , and where be your 
 kraal ? tell we , that we may know with whom we have to deal . " 

 " my name be Umbopa . I be of the Zulu people , yet not of they . the house 
 of my tribe be in the far north ; it be leave behind when the Zulus come 
 down here a ' thousand year ago , ' long before Chaka reign in 
 Zululand . I have no kraal . I have wander for many year . I come from 
 the North as a child to Zululand . I be Cetewayo 's man in the 
 Nkomabakosi Regiment , serve there under the great Captain , 
 Umslopogaasi of the Axe , 1   who teach my hand to fight . afterwards I 
 run away from Zululand and come to Natal because I want to see the 
 white man 's way . next I fight against Cetewayo in the war . since then 
 I have be work in Natal . now I be tired , and would go North again . 
 here be not my place . I want no money , but I be a brave man , and be 
 worth my place and meat . I have speak . " 

 I be rather puzzle by this man and his way of speech . it be evident 
 to I from his manner that in the main he be tell the truth , but 
 somehow he seem different from the ordinary run of Zulus , and I 
 rather mistrust his offer to come without pay . be in a difficulty , 
 I translate his word to Sir Henry and Good , and ask they their 
 opinion . 

 Sir Henry tell I to ask he to stand up . Umbopa do so , at the same 
 time slip off the long military great coat which he wear , and 
 reveal himself naked except for the moocha round his centre and a 
 necklace of lion ' claw . certainly he be a magnificent - look man ; I 
 never see a fine native . stand about six foot three high he be 
 broad in proportion , and very shapely . in that light , too , his skin 
 look scarcely more than dark , except here and there where deep black 
 scar mark old assegai wound . Sir Henry walk up to he and look 
 into his proud , handsome face . 

 " they make a good pair , do not   they ? " say good ; " one as big as the 
 other . " 

 " I like your look , Mr. Umbopa , and I will take you as my servant , " 
 say Sir Henry in English . 

 Umbopa evidently understand he , for he answer in Zulu , " it be well " ; 
 and then add , with a glance at the white man 's great stature and 
 breadth , " we be man , thou and I. "
